  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_12f89799-f2ba-4201-8a2f-c857f446ec8a'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/27867823-christophe"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1464382226i/27867823._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/27867823-christophe">Christophe</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">For romance, you have to wait for the second book and the third. In the first, it is true that there is little romance. It is mostly about chivalry, s<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">For romance, you have to wait for the second book and the third. In the first, it is true that there is little romance. It is mostly about chivalry, setting the world, the plot and the characters.<br />If you want romance though (not only for that because this is a great book for several reasons), it is worth to read this cycle in its entirety.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_486452225" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/486452225" />
    <a title="Dirk Grobbelaar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar"><img alt="Dirk Grobbelaar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545117991p2/3102740.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/486452225?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dirk Grobbelaar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dirk Grobbelaar" href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar">Dirk Grobbelaar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer486452225" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16245647503420016658"><i>'You'd better get ready to ride, Sparhawk.'</i><br/><br/>Meet Sparhawk: Pandion Knight and Queen’s Champion. If this were D&D his character class would likely be Paladin; he can cast spells, but mostly prefers to just chop off heads with his considerable broadsword.<br/><br/>Sparhawk is a bit of a bad-ass. So is his horse, Faran.<br/><br/><i>'It's the Queen's Champion. Don't ever stand in his way.'</i><br/><br/>I fairly devoured this novel in short order. Sparhawk has a zero tolerance approach to BS which is immensely refreshing and enjoyable. <br/><br/>T</span>
  <span id="freeText16245647503420016658" style="display:none"><i>'You'd better get ready to ride, Sparhawk.'</i><br /><br />Meet Sparhawk: Pandion Knight and Queen’s Champion. If this were D&amp;D his character class would likely be Paladin; he can cast spells, but mostly prefers to just chop off heads with his considerable broadsword.<br /><br />Sparhawk is a bit of a bad-ass. So is his horse, Faran.<br /><br /><i>'It's the Queen's Champion. Don't ever stand in his way.'</i><br /><br />I fairly devoured this novel in short order. Sparhawk has a zero tolerance approach to BS which is immensely refreshing and enjoyable. <br /><br />The Diamond Throne is a no-nonsense tale. The writing style on display here is sometimes a bit workmanlike and without literary flourish, but it seems to suit the story (and especially the protagonist) rather well.<br /><br /><i>'It's going to be dangerous.'</i><br /><br />And thus our hero sets out on a quest to save the kingdom, accompanied by a few companions.<br /><br />Ah, the tried and tested recipe for a fantasy story. Fortunately it’s hardly possible to make a mess of a book when it has such an endearing protagonist. Eddings seems to have struck gold with Sparhawk, because I was willing to forgive all kinds of faults.<br /><br />It’s an old school story that (in my opinion) leans slightly more toward the heroic fantasy of Gemmell than it does the high fantasy of Tolkien, and yet it still sits somewhere in between. I haven’t read <b>The Belgariad</b> yet, so I’m unable to draw any comparison. Eddings, it seems, was somewhat hot property in the 1980s, alongside the likes of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8588.Raymond_E__Feist" title="Raymond E. Feist" rel="nofollow noopener">Raymond E. Feist</a>. Just don’t expect dragons or elves.<br /><br /><i> 'What are you planning, Sparhawk?'<br />'Chastisement.’</i><br /><br />One of the major selling points of this story is the dialogue. It’s rather good, what. In fact, it’s absolutely delightful more often than not.<br /><br />The bantering between the characters, and especially the knights of the different orders, is deftly dealt with. It’s remarkable that, for all the humour in this novel, it never stoops to being silly or a parody.<br /><br /><i>'It should be almost like a cheese grater when we start to grind them up against your walls.'<br />'And I can drop some interesting things on them from my battlements as well. Arrows, large rocks, burning pitch - that sort of thing.'<br />'We're going to get on splendidly, my Lord,'</i><br /><br />The story won me over with its simple charm. It’s not as complicated and reality-ridden as the modern fantasy, but it does have a bit of dirt under its fingernails. <br />There are some religious and philosophical leanings, but for the most part these serve to underscore elements of the story.<br /><br /><i>'Has he ever bitten you?'<br />'Once. Then I explained to him that I'd rather he didn't do it any more.'<br />'Explained?'<br />'I used a stout stick. He got the idea almost immediately.'</i><br /><br />In short: it’s a fantasy adventure that should appeal to readers that don’t have a very straight literary stick wedged somewhere tight.<br /><br />For literary merit, cleverness and all that jazz: 3.5 stars<br />For pure unadulterated entertainment value: 4.5 stars <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="16245647503420016658" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_11482551" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11482551" />
    <a title="Denise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/732847-denise"><img alt="Denise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199318917p2/732847.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/11482551?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Denise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Denise" href="/user/show/732847-denise">Denise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of High Fantasy series who are willing to sit through a slow opening novel</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/732847-denise?shelf=reread">reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/732847-denise?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer11482551" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4663861351937758686">David Eddings is comfort reading of the highest order (okay, I will admit that I stopped reading him after a certain point, but this series was before that point, so!); I'd reread all of the rest of this series in December, and all of the followup series (The Tamuli), but my copy of this one had taken a walkabout, so I had to backfill and re-read the first one last.<br/><br/>Eddings writes High Fantasy (with Capital Letters, even), with all the tropes and archetypes thereof -- there aren't any surprises h</span>
  <span id="freeText4663861351937758686" style="display:none">David Eddings is comfort reading of the highest order (okay, I will admit that I stopped reading him after a certain point, but this series was before that point, so!); I'd reread all of the rest of this series in December, and all of the followup series (The Tamuli), but my copy of this one had taken a walkabout, so I had to backfill and re-read the first one last.<br /><br />Eddings writes High Fantasy (with Capital Letters, even), with all the tropes and archetypes thereof -- there aren't any surprises here. And this is surprisingly slow-moving for the first book in a new world; he spends a lot of time setting up structures and relationships that would have been pretty obvious in context, and he's always had a little bit of a problem with over-expositing. (I suspect that it would actually be possible for a reader who's familiar with the genre to pick up book two, start there, and be perfectly fine.) It isn't that the book itself moves slowly, but that it takes a long time for many of the elements of the larger plot to come into play.<br /><br />On the other hand, the characters are archetypically-familiar without being cardboard cutouts, the main character's just a darling, and even though Eddings's prose is clunky in places, his ability to juggle multiple strands of plot is a delight to watch on a technical level and just plain interesting on the readerly level. If you're looking for an old-familiar-friend series, give this one a try. As archetypical High Fantasy goes, this one's a keeper.</span>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780586203729" href="/book/show/983172.The_Diamond_Throne">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer506567434&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer506567434" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6224953651732874837">The Diamond Throne kicks off with a bang with the main character returning after a ten year unjust exile at the behest of the new Queen with only one little snag in that the queen has taken seriously ill and had had to put into a form of stasis and this begins a quest to find a cure for the young Queen! :D Though there are also the parties responsible for poisoning intent on causing trouble which of course leads to all sorts of attempted upsets, daring do's, plenty of clenched teeth and grim chi</span>
  <span id="freeText6224953651732874837" style="display:none">The Diamond Throne kicks off with a bang with the main character returning after a ten year unjust exile at the behest of the new Queen with only one little snag in that the queen has taken seriously ill and had had to put into a form of stasis and this begins a quest to find a cure for the young Queen! :D Though there are also the parties responsible for poisoning intent on causing trouble which of course leads to all sorts of attempted upsets, daring do's, plenty of clenched teeth and grim chins! :D<br /><br />Characterisation is excellent with all the characters having distinctly different personalities which results in a lot of humour that ties in with all the action and creates a brilliant set of situations and interactions that will keep you guessing which the way things are going g to go with the plot! :D There are also parts where it appear that the characters are having a bit of a holiday which again adds to the tone of the book! :D<br /><br />The Diamond Throne does a great job of setting up the world of The Elenium with us been introduced to the order of the various Knights as well as the political structure that is heavily based on mediaeval Europe's feudal culture that with a few more democratic processes in place! But this adds to a great cambers for the adventures of the characters throughout the book which leads to encounters with many people from different nations as well as giving an opportunity to explore the past history of the world and some the major events that happened to have formed the societies and Nations! :D<br /><br />The characters are all really well depicted as being different from each other with no characters actually feeling like hot copies of the other and all the characters regardless of rank, or how often they peer, seems to be lacking in three-dimensional characterisation which leads to some great interactions between them and really adds to the feel of the book especially when the characters are in conflict with each other but the come together in order to overcome the obstacles are often thrown in their way! You also get to see a lots of the past history of the land and the characters which also adds to the overall palimpsest that is depicted throughout the book! :D<br /><br />Throughout the book you can see in and detect that there is a much bigger plotline going on and this is obviously the set up for the later books showing very clearly that there is a lot more going on very at first see in this opening book! :D Though this is not to say that The Diamond Throne is not completely packed out with events and world building you can see why three books were needed to get this world started! This all adds to a adventure which never lets up at all for a second with the characters getting into once great after another! :D<br /><br />Face paced, lots of humour, great plot, world building and noble questing! :D Highly recommended! :D</span>

<div id="review_241438828" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/241438828" />
    <a title="Kathryn Ford" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7081484-kathryn-ford"><img alt="Kathryn Ford" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575710913p2/7081484.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/241438828?book_show_action=true">Nov 30, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathryn Ford" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathryn Ford" href="/user/show/7081484-kathryn-ford">Kathryn Ford</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7081484-kathryn-ford?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7081484-kathryn-ford?shelf=books-i-reread">books-i-reread</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7081484-kathryn-ford?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer241438828" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13121336320282292554">The Diamond Throne is the first book in the Elenium series, and trust me it is amazing! It is followed by The Ruby Knight and The Sapphire Rose. This is possibly one of my most favourite books! I love absolutely everything about it! I could write pages and pages on how brilliant this book is, but since that would take you too long to read and I would land up giving to much away, I’ve tried my best to explain why this book is so fantastic.<br/>David Eddings is more well known for his Bolgariad series,</span>
  <span id="freeText13121336320282292554" style="display:none">The Diamond Throne is the first book in the Elenium series, and trust me it is amazing! It is followed by The Ruby Knight and The Sapphire Rose. This is possibly one of my most favourite books! I love absolutely everything about it! I could write pages and pages on how brilliant this book is, but since that would take you too long to read and I would land up giving to much away, I’ve tried my best to explain why this book is so fantastic.<br />David Eddings is more well known for his Bolgariad series, which is a coming of age Epic Fantasy.<br />The Elenium is unique in that it is not a coming of age series. The characters are already old and seasoned. To be honest, I found the Elenium to be a much better series than The Bolgariad. The characters have more depth, the story has more layers and subplots. The magic system is more complex and the religious structure is more unique and multilayered. The creatures are also more interesting. This series is action packed and often quite gorey.<br /><br />The Diamond Throne begins with Sparhawk, a knight of the Pandion Order, arriving back home from ten years of enforced exile. His arrival is in the dark and the wet, a perfect setting! Sparhawk is already middle aged, cinical and world weary, he is unattractive and far from your usual kind of hero. He is loyal to the crown and his Knightly Order, but his moral cumpus is often somewhat skewed and his behaviour not very Noble. He is however a character that you instantly like, he has flaws, he has made mistakes in the past and continues to make them. He is a steadfast friend, he shows kindness to those in need. When Sparhawk has a purpose, he will do everything in his power to fulfil it. <br /><br />To read my full review please click:<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.bookrantorrave.com/blog/the-diamond-throne-rave">http://www.bookrantorrave.com/blog/th...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13121336320282292554" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_178085300" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/178085300" />
    <a title="Boingboing" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5405500-boingboing"><img alt="Boingboing" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1305383833p2/5405500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/178085300?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Boingboing" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Boingboing" href="/user/show/5405500-boingboing">Boingboing</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer178085300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12090091970126459743">Stating this upfront. It's not genius writing. The characters are two dimensional and perfect. They look perfect (except for when they have a feature that turns them from perfect to unique), they act perfect, their motives are noble and honorable, except for the villains who twirl their mustaches and laugh at their maniacalness. <br/><br/>Okay, maybe not quite that bad but it's close. Every cliche that could be hit seemed like it was.<br/><br/> No it didn't wrap up the main conflict. There don't seem to be any subp</span>
  <span id="freeText12090091970126459743" style="display:none">Stating this upfront. It's not genius writing. The characters are two dimensional and perfect. They look perfect (except for when they have a feature that turns them from perfect to unique), they act perfect, their motives are noble and honorable, except for the villains who twirl their mustaches and laugh at their maniacalness. <br /><br />Okay, maybe not quite that bad but it's close. Every cliche that could be hit seemed like it was.<br /><br /> No it didn't wrap up the main conflict. There don't seem to be any subplots of any importance to give depth or breadth to the story or the characters. Just one plot, straight away, and it's not concluded with any degree of satisfaction, no doubt to get you to buy the next book.<br /><br />Despite all that, I still loved this book. It's light reading, it's fast reading. It's not taxing on the brain or the believability. It's a fun read. It's escapist. For all the popcorn, I'd read it again and plan on reading the other two books. <br /><br />Don't look for brilliant writing, deep characters, wickedly twisted plots. Watch for the low flying Mary Sues that come at you from every corner and enjoy the guilty pleasure reading for what it is. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12090091970126459743" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15605320451510550562">I’m cheating here a little bit because this is a three book series, but it seems a little redundant to only talk about the first one, so please view this review as being for all three. <br/>David Eddings is one of the great masters of the quest fantasy. What Tolkien started many, many authors have run with, though few as successfully or just generally as satisfyingly as Eddings.<br/>The Elenium focuses on a Knight called Sparhawk and his travels to save his young and very beautiful queen, who is encased i</span>
  <span id="freeText15605320451510550562" style="display:none">I’m cheating here a little bit because this is a three book series, but it seems a little redundant to only talk about the first one, so please view this review as being for all three. <br />David Eddings is one of the great masters of the quest fantasy. What Tolkien started many, many authors have run with, though few as successfully or just generally as satisfyingly as Eddings.<br />The Elenium focuses on a Knight called Sparhawk and his travels to save his young and very beautiful queen, who is encased in diamond to keep her alive. Of course, Sparhawk is joined on his voyage by a number of others, all possessing severe likeability (my review, my words ;) and suitable heroism. They are wonderfully easy to read and contain enough cynicism to keep them from spilling over into cheese. <br /><br />So, the short version is, if you like quests, swords, true love, magic and knights, read this.<br /></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/901342-jacob?shelf=from_library">from_library</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer997436221" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7147286481616425798">I remember this series very fondly, but I think I enjoyed it more as a teenager. It's still a good read, but the plot is a bit too "go here; chase clue; repeat". Subtlety is not Eddings' strong point. Like <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35921.On_Basilisk_Station__Honor_Harrington___1_' title='On Basilisk Station (Honor Harrington, #1) by David Weber' rel='nofollow noopener'>On Basilisk Station</a>, the humor is a bit too on-the-nose, the protagonists are a bit too capable, and the author relishes showing antagonists getting their comeuppance. The story very much doesn't stand on its own (which has become a pet peeve of mine). And the prologue is a fictitious legend t</span>
  <span id="freeText7147286481616425798" style="display:none">I remember this series very fondly, but I think I enjoyed it more as a teenager. It's still a good read, but the plot is a bit too "go here; chase clue; repeat". Subtlety is not Eddings' strong point. Like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35921.On_Basilisk_Station__Honor_Harrington___1_" title="On Basilisk Station (Honor Harrington, #1) by David Weber" rel="nofollow noopener">On Basilisk Station</a>, the humor is a bit too on-the-nose, the protagonists are a bit too capable, and the author relishes showing antagonists getting their comeuppance. The story very much doesn't stand on its own (which has become a pet peeve of mine). And the prologue is a fictitious legend that runs rampant over xkcd's "too many fantasy names" <a target="blank" href="https://xkcd.com/483/" rel="nofollow noopener">rule of thumb</a>.<br /><br />Those are mostly quibbles and they may not be a problem for you. The setting here is very detailed and the characters are mostly plausible, even if they do quip back and forth like they're starring in a sitcom. I didn't remember the big reveal near the end and was surprised all over again. I suspect I remember more of the sequels, so I'll be continuing the trilogy :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="7147286481616425798" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer79263958917957488">This is either my 3rd or 4th reading. Admittedly, it has some of the flaws of most of Eddings' work: the characters are chuckleheads and the plot usually revolves around...well, I won't spoil it but you can probably figure it out.<br/><br/>However, there are some marked improvements (and in the realm of general fantasy it is better than most).<br/><br/>1. Sparhawk is infinitely superior to Garion.<br/>2. The bad guys are far more interesting than in the Belgariad. Azash is truly something to be feared. Kal Torak, by con</span>
  <span id="freeText79263958917957488" style="display:none">This is either my 3rd or 4th reading. Admittedly, it has some of the flaws of most of Eddings' work: the characters are chuckleheads and the plot usually revolves around...well, I won't spoil it but you can probably figure it out.<br /><br />However, there are some marked improvements (and in the realm of general fantasy it is better than most).<br /><br />1. Sparhawk is infinitely superior to Garion.<br />2. The bad guys are far more interesting than in the Belgariad. Azash is truly something to be feared. Kal Torak, by contrast, was a glorified form of Vader.<br /><br />Perhaps something else to be noticed: there is a connection between the health and wholeness of the monarch and the health of the land.<br /><br />This is probably Eddings' best work. Highly recommended.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5113736624690259804">Not the correct edition. Mine is an old library copy from cassette tapes.   <span id="freeText5113736624690259804" style="display:none">Not the correct edition. Mine is an old library copy from cassette tapes. The sound quality kind of sucked, but I've read the book in paper several times, so it wasn't a huge deal.<br /><br />This is a simple story, a fun romp, that doesn't take itself too seriously. It's an interesting world with a neat system of magic &amp; a definite goal. The characters are pretty standard, but their dialog makes them more interesting. I love that Faran, Sparhawk's horse, plays such a great role.<br /><br />It definitely ends on a cliff hanger, so be prepared to read the next two books. I liked this trilogy better than the sequel trilogy.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17885423711721699614">I suspect that the high regard in which I held Edding's writing had more to do with the context of my life at the time that I initially discovered his work (starved for distraction) because... this, at least, really wasn't very good. And filled with disparaging and unnecessary references toward women. It is possible that this is just the very worst of his efforts, but... I think I'll have to stand by the past tense "held," in my opening sentence, as far as that <i>high</i> regard is concerned. </span>

<div id="review_202680158" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202680158" />
    <a title="Wastrel" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5743748-wastrel"><img alt="Wastrel" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317660831p2/5743748.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202680158?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wastrel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wastrel" href="/user/show/5743748-wastrel">Wastrel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=r-ps">r-ps</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5743748-wastrel?shelf=fantastika-epic-fantasy">fantastika-epic-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202680158" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8532460290117747866">On the one hand, I want to give credit to Eddings for trying to write this. He went from family-friendly bestselling non-Tolkien epic fantasy author of all time (at the time) to, immediately, a dark and brutal semi-noir proto-grimdark story about church politics, drenched in sex (albeit off-screen) and blood and elder gods and incest and pederasty and fountains of blood and ethnic cleansing and blood. And when I was eight, I loved it, although given his massive decline in sales apparently a lot </span>
  <span id="freeText8532460290117747866" style="display:none">On the one hand, I want to give credit to Eddings for trying to write this. He went from family-friendly bestselling non-Tolkien epic fantasy author of all time (at the time) to, immediately, a dark and brutal semi-noir proto-grimdark story about church politics, drenched in sex (albeit off-screen) and blood and elder gods and incest and pederasty and fountains of blood and ethnic cleansing and blood. And when I was eight, I loved it, although given his massive decline in sales apparently a lot of other people didn't.<br /><br />On the other hand, although he tried to write this, he didn't do a great job of it. It's far too rushed (more plot in 400 pages than in half of the Wheel of Time), Eddings just isn't a great author, and his long-term latent tendency toward racism, conservativism and sub-Nietzschean contempt for the little people breaches into plain sight in a way that doesn't exactly spoil the book (it's a colour around the edges, not the central picture) but that does give it a bad aftertaste. Perhaps most seriously, he's unable to fully commit to the grimdark, and the natural buoyancy of his work, floating back whenever not held down to a bubbly sort of smug 'what a lark' nonchalance, undermines the better and more serious bits.<br /><br />Which is a shame, because there are actually some good ideas here, and once I got into it I found myself actually quite enjoying it. Enough, at least, that I'll probably go on and re-read the next two volumes. <br /><br />So it's not very good. In fact, it's bad. But it's not ALL bad, and those interested in the history of the genre may find it worth the trouble, although I'd hardly go around recommending it to people on literary merit alone.<br /><br /><a href="http://vacuouswastrel.wordpress.com/2014/03/03/the-diamond-throne-by-david-eddings/" rel="nofollow noopener">My far, far longer review can be found over here.</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8532460290117747866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_25029673" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/25029673" />
    <a title="Elizabeth Wallace" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1142024-elizabeth-wallace"><img alt="Elizabeth Wallace" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536676925p2/1142024.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/25029673?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth Wallace" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth Wallace" href="/user/show/1142024-elizabeth-wallace">Elizabeth Wallace</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1142024-elizabeth-wallace?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer25029673" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15419596177146443568">The dialog in this book is probably the best thing about it. Eddings has a WONDERFUL talent with snappy remarks and clever comebacks. I remember when I first read it, reading bits aloud to my sister. "But ThPARhawk" he lisped. "You know how thatin thpoths when it geths wet!!" <br/><br/>Yeah, probably doesn't mean much to you, but trust me, it was hilarious.<br/><br/>It's a true fantasy novel, with knights and magic and battles and the works. Definitely my favorite Eddings novel.<br/><br/>That being said...<br/><br/>After rereading it </span>
  <span id="freeText15419596177146443568" style="display:none">The dialog in this book is probably the best thing about it. Eddings has a WONDERFUL talent with snappy remarks and clever comebacks. I remember when I first read it, reading bits aloud to my sister. "But ThPARhawk" he lisped. "You know how thatin thpoths when it geths wet!!" <br /><br />Yeah, probably doesn't mean much to you, but trust me, it was hilarious.<br /><br />It's a true fantasy novel, with knights and magic and battles and the works. Definitely my favorite Eddings novel.<br /><br />That being said...<br /><br />After rereading it recently, what really jumped out at me was that it needed tighter editing. In searching for a really dramatic phrase, Eddings sometimes got a liiiiittle bit wordy, unnecessarily so. At one point he says "The battle went on for another ten minutes, and then it was over." As opposed to...what? "The battle went on for another ten minutes...and then it went on for a little longer?" There's examples like that all over the book.<br /><br />And oh my GOD, his editor needed to tell him "If you use the word 'bleak' one more time I will cram this thesaurus down your throat." I'm not kidding guys, in this book and the one that follows you can find the word "bleak" or "bleakly" in every. Single. Chapter. Not kidding, every one. One time he used it THREE TIMES in the same chapter. That kinda thing reeeeaaally starts to get annoying, because it jerks you out of the moment. "Yes, OKAY, bleak again, you couldn't have used ANY OTHER WORD...?" Definitely didn't notice that the first time around, sure noticed it now/.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15419596177146443568" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a title="Suzanne (Under the Covers Book blog)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Suzanne (Under the Covers Book blog)" href="/user/show/3575839-suzanne-under-the-covers-book-blog">Suzanne (Under the Covers Book blog)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780246134486" href="/book/show/983173.The_Diamond_Throne">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3575839-suzanne-under-the-covers-book-blog?shelf=author-david-eddings">author-david-eddings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3575839-suzanne-under-the-covers-book-blog?shelf=genre-fantasy">genre-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3575839-suzanne-under-the-covers-book-blog?shelf=mages-witches-magic-users">mages-witches-magic-users</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3575839-suzanne-under-the-covers-book-blog?shelf=finished-series">finished-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3575839-suzanne-under-the-covers-book-blog?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3575839-suzanne-under-the-covers-book-blog?shelf=funny-funny-har-har">funny-funny-har-har</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3575839-suzanne-under-the-covers-book-blog?shelf=mythology-gods-and-goddesses">mythology-gods-and-goddesses</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3575839-suzanne-under-the-covers-book-blog?shelf=action-adventure">action-adventure</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer111284814" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7175176145576558475">David Eddings' Belgariad series is what first gave me my love of reading, especially of fantasy, so I always have a soft spot for anything he has written and this book is no exception. Although I think the Belgariad is better I thoroughly enjoyed this book.<br/><br/>Sir Sparhawk, a Knight of the Pandion order has returened to Elenia after 10 years of being exciled to find that the young queen, Ehlena, is sick and dying, betrayed by a corrupt Churchman who has lofty ambitions and her bastard cousin. It is </span>
  <span id="freeText7175176145576558475" style="display:none">David Eddings' Belgariad series is what first gave me my love of reading, especially of fantasy, so I always have a soft spot for anything he has written and this book is no exception. Although I think the Belgariad is better I thoroughly enjoyed this book.<br /><br />Sir Sparhawk, a Knight of the Pandion order has returened to Elenia after 10 years of being exciled to find that the young queen, Ehlena, is sick and dying, betrayed by a corrupt Churchman who has lofty ambitions and her bastard cousin. It is up to Sparhawk to try and find a cure for her illness, with the help of his friends and fellow knights, as well as a thief and a girl-child, before Queen Ehlena dies and the world is thrown into an age of darkness.<br /><br />What I love about David Eddings is that he creates such likable characters, reading about Sparhawk, Kurik, Kalten and company is always very entertaining, the banter between them all is funny and seems to be one of his key tools in chracterization. However, the characters to bare a resemblance to his characters in the Belgariad, the story is also quite similar.<br /><br />But the plot is different enough to keep it interesting, despite certain similarities, and is very fast paced, Sparhawk is constantly on the move, and it seems once one crisis is averted another one pops up and Sparkhawk is trotting off to sort that out as well.<br /><br />This is a fun read, it isn't a dark and involved fantasy, but rather a great adventure story full of knights and magic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7175176145576558475" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_60277547" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Jak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1044380-jak"><img alt="Jak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207055562p2/1044380.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer60277547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13602996511935177037">The Diamond Throne starts the Elenium trilogy and centre around the hero Sir Sparhawk who is the exiled Queen’s champion from an order of knights who are trained in magic as they are combat. On returning from exile Sparhawk finds that his queen has been poisoned and her power usurped by the corrupt head of the Elenian church. However the sorceress Serephina has encased the queen in diamond in order to delay the effects of the poison and buy Sparhawk time to find a cure. <br/><br/>Sparhawk sets about gathe</span>
  <span id="freeText13602996511935177037" style="display:none">The Diamond Throne starts the Elenium trilogy and centre around the hero Sir Sparhawk who is the exiled Queen’s champion from an order of knights who are trained in magic as they are combat. On returning from exile Sparhawk finds that his queen has been poisoned and her power usurped by the corrupt head of the Elenian church. However the sorceress Serephina has encased the queen in diamond in order to delay the effects of the poison and buy Sparhawk time to find a cure. <br /><br />Sparhawk sets about gathering a team representing the four knight orders and sets out on his quest to find an antidote to the queen’s poison.<br /><br />In the Belgraion and Sparhawk series of books Eddings tells superb stories and builds deeply complex and rich characters that you can’t help but connect with and root for. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13602996511935177037" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_115272229" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/115272229" />
    <a title="Tom Hansen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3788809-tom-hansen"><img alt="Tom Hansen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1542648926p2/3788809.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/115272229?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Hansen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Hansen" href="/user/show/3788809-tom-hansen">Tom Hansen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3788809-tom-hansen?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3788809-tom-hansen?shelf=political">political</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3788809-tom-hansen?shelf=religious">religious</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3788809-tom-hansen?shelf=2010-read">2010-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer115272229" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15503965121650861435">An exiled knight returns after 10 years to find his queen deathly ill and encased in magic stone in order to prolong her life. He sets out on a highly political and violent quest to rout out who is behind her mysterious illness and thwart those people that would want to usurp her throne.<br/><br/>I enjoyed this book. It wasn’t earth-shattering, but it was a fun read. It was also a fast-read. I made it through it’s 435 pages in 6 hours or so.<br/><br/>What enjoyed the most would be some of the quotes. Eddings made s</span>
  <span id="freeText15503965121650861435" style="display:none">An exiled knight returns after 10 years to find his queen deathly ill and encased in magic stone in order to prolong her life. He sets out on a highly political and violent quest to rout out who is behind her mysterious illness and thwart those people that would want to usurp her throne.<br /><br />I enjoyed this book. It wasn’t earth-shattering, but it was a fun read. It was also a fast-read. I made it through it’s 435 pages in 6 hours or so.<br /><br />What enjoyed the most would be some of the quotes. Eddings made sure the main characters were constantly bickering and bantering back and forth, throwing out jabs and one liners at each other. This kept the story light and I found myself laughing out loud (quite literally) at least a dozen times during the read of this book.<br /><br />He also had some deep entrenching religions in his world that ultimately believed in the same gods, but different aspects of the gods, much like we do now. This led to some interesting relationships among some of the characters. The constant proselytizing by one of the religions did get a little old, but religion is integral to the storyline, and I’m hoping the reasons why will be fleshed out later.<br /><br />I had a rough time with the names. There must have been at least 7o different names (people as well as places) to keep track of all within it’s pages. This got confusing. I ended up concentrating on the core group of adventurers and glossed over details of who they were speaking to. <br /><br />It still left me not fully understanding the intricacies of the dialogue. I followed the conversations while they were happening and by the time we came back to the same character later in the book I had no mental connection to their previous conversations. This made for some confusing conversations later on in the book when the author did not introduce the character, instead relying on me having kept up with his myriad of names.<br /><br />I liked that traveling took some time. One does not simply hop on a horse and by the end of the day be at another town on the other side of the country. They travelled for days, sometimes weeks to get around. Much of this was sped up with a couple vignettes during stops to describe the heros as they worked their way across the land. Because of the speed-up during traveling I lost sense of time. The entire book takes place over the course of quite a few months but I didn’t feel like it really did. It felt more like a couple weeks.<br /><br />As I neared the end of the book I wondered how they were going to wrap up the entire storyline that quickly, and they ultimately did not. This book was written as part of a trilogy and while plenty happens in the book, nothing really is resolved by the end and the hero is given the knowledge that will launch into the next two books.<br /><br />Overall I had fun reading it. It was fast-paced, witty, and engaging. The multitude of characters that came in and out of the story made for a confusing read and the disappointing ending to this book made for a bit of a let-down. I would recommend it if you are committed to reading the rest of the trilogy, which I am currently working on now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15503965121650861435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14684440-brynn?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14684440-brynn?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2067854607" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer706499766189137136">I asked someone to buy me this book in grade seven, if memory serves. I had read and loved Eddings' other series The Belgariad by that point- Pawn of Prophecy was the first novel I ever read by myself, giving it a special, permanent place in my heart - and I was eager to read something else from the guy who, at the time, was my favorite fantasy writer (and the only one I knew, which would be why). I read maybe a third of The Diamond Throne before realizing I didn't care; and I stuffed it, disapp</span>
  <span id="freeText706499766189137136" style="display:none">I asked someone to buy me this book in grade seven, if memory serves. I had read and loved Eddings' other series The Belgariad by that point- Pawn of Prophecy was the first novel I ever read by myself, giving it a special, permanent place in my heart - and I was eager to read something else from the guy who, at the time, was my favorite fantasy writer (and the only one I knew, which would be why). I read maybe a third of The Diamond Throne before realizing I didn't care; and I stuffed it, disappointed, back on my shelf - where it sat for eight years. <br />A couple months ago I saw it there and decided to give it another chance. I was feeling nostalgic and light-hearted or something, I guess; lately I've been trying to make myself read heavy academic stuff and thoughtful prescient fiction like The Handmaid's Tale - and when you do that to yourself for a long enough period of time, you really start to keenly crave the opposite, in the same way that a month of vegan food makes you want chip wagon fries. There's nothing wrong with that. Sometimes you have to give yourself a break or you go mad - when you find yourself struggling through an economics textbook because you vaguely feel as if you "should" be reading it? Probably time to have a breather, to let your brain unwind, calm down from the forced hyperdrive.<br /><br />That's what I was looking for, I think, in this book - and really that's pretty much what I got. Eddings is good with the pulpy, satisfying, sword and sorcery stuff - it's not brainless, in fact it's obvious he was a smart guy who thought about smart things - but it is pulpy. It's high camp, with some philosophy hanging off the sides. I don't even mean that in a dismissive or condescending way: I think camp with bits of philosophy attached is probably the single most effective way to tackle high fantasy; because the genre is ridiculous from the outset (magic, swords, knights, dragons, princesses, mcguffins, prophecies), and if you don't at least make some effort to recognize that you're going to end up with something completely insufferable (unless you're George R. R. Martin, of course, who takes high fantasy extremely seriously and makes it outstanding). This kind of stuff, generally speaking, needs to be at least marginally aware of its basic absurdity. I always got the feeling Eddings was very aware of it. If you have nothing else in your story except camp then it's just camp, and hard to be invested in. Fun, but ultimately going nowhere. When you add a dash of something interesting - big ideas about the universe, or something - then you have something solid to hold onto, as a reader. Something interesting to keep reading for. Because gruff knights and magic mcguffins get tiresome very quickly, if there's nothing else but them. <br />You can also go too far with this, obviously, and have your philosophy attempts get really overbearing and pretentious - lots of books and other media fall into this trap. So to reiterate here... For me at least, the best sort of high fantasy is the kind that balances camp and intellectual depth just enough to be entertaining, just enough to be meaningful - and not too much to be overbearing. In the past, Eddings has been able to do this. <br /><br />I think he also does it with this book - barely. The camp is there, and it's silly, but he knows it's silly - and there's enough general cleverness to just about make up for the stupid parts (of which there are many, though they are mostly quiet, relatively unobtrusive stupid parts, hidden away in bits of dialogue, peppered throughout action sequences). The dialogue is so idiosyncratic that it could be used for an example in an English textbook. Eddings has exactly one setting for his dialogue: dry, repetitious humor. Every character - every single one - shares the same wry tone, and expresses themselves in the same speaking pattern (clearly the inner voice of Eddings himself). Sometimes it's appreciably clever, but other times it's insufferable. The characters DO have marginal differences, beyond their physical appearances, but none of them escape being witty (except for a single person, the knight Bevier, who is the lone prudish, earnestly humorless goodboy in this fantasy universe, apparently. Even he however stoops into wit a few times; and he doesn't play a very big part). <br /><br />The book is dominated, quite literally in fact, by the person of Sparhawk, our main character. Here's the thing: he's either a stupendously clever, subversive commentary on toxic masculinity, or just plain old toxic masculinity as described by an author who very much seems to admire those qualities in a man. The wonderful thing about David Eddings' books is that there's always a woman character whose authority the big masculine men defer to - and that's why this Sparhawk situation is so complicated. On the one hand, within the first chapter or two Eddings writes a scene wherein big strong manly Sparhawk encounters a very effeminate, implicitly gay nobleman, and threatens to hurt him if ever the nobleman touches him. This encounter, to me, came off absolutely viciously homophobic. Sparhawk, as the book opens, is seen to be a kind person - he gives a whore out in the rain some coins and tells her gently to go buy herself something to eat and get in out of the cold. Not a few pages later, Sparhawk is threatening a coded-gay character, saying if he touches him he'll hurt him. (There is also a pedophile character who has a brief appearance, described as being very effeminate and a molester of young boys. Put these together and you get an undeniable case for homophobia). <br />So: Eddings has made himself clear. Femininity in men is bad. Masculinity? Good. As evidenced by our hero Sparhawk, who literally muscles his way through every obstacle he is faced with throughout the opening chapters, dominating all the lesser humans he encounters, being the textbook example of a bully in a position of power abusing that; and yet. And yet, Sparhawk defers to the tiny woman sorcerer Sephrenia - arguably the best character (certainly the most likable one). Sephrenia (who is also a carbon copy of Polgara from Eddings' other series, but never mind) is herself not really portrayed as very feminine - which is interesting. She is clearly no sort of sexualized, not even in the remotest way; that's not why she's in the story, just like that's not why Sparhawk is. And I think that's good. But there seems to be an almost hypocritical disconnect here - that Eddings can write such good female characters and (it was always my impression) take an honest crack at being feminist, while also being obviously homophobic - and also... racist. Yeah... that's the next part. <br /><br />It's a recurring theme in his books that the non-white people are the bad guys. In the Belgariad/Mallorean, the bad guys were the Murgos, who were described as sort of Oriental-looking. "Angular" eyes and all that. In this series, the Rendors - basically explicitly described as Arab - are stupid, dirty, inferior people. Eddings makes a deal of fuss over what he calls "racial" differences - apparently, the Styric people (of which Sephrenia is one) are less sophisticated than the white Elenes (of which Sparhawk, the hero, is one). This point is brought up many times, just in case you weren't uncomfortable enough already. As is the case with Eddings being somehow almost feminist while simultaneously fetishizing toxic masculinity, he also seems to be aware of his own racism, even while he goes on about "racial" traits and treats them - as far as I can tell - as if they really are a thing that exists. It strikes me as less commentary and more a thing he actually believes is true. Weirdly, like I said, he does seem aware - but his version of being aware is to create exceptions to the rule of a race being inferior, like with Sephrenia, who is wonderful, while also belonging to the Styric race, who are literally described many times as being less sophisticated (stupider) than the Elenes. <br />I get the sense with David Eddings that he was just sort of born in the wrong time to be as progressive as he always sort of leaned towards being. He was born in 1931; The Diamond Throne was written in the 80s. I don't know if this is me trying to make excuses for him (probably) but I really do believe that all this racism and homophobia would have been mightily toned down, or non-existent, if he'd been born a little later. Or maybe not. Maybe I just want that to be true. But I'm thinking, if he can be feminist like he was (his own version of feminist, anyway, which is more "dude trying to be good" rather than "dude who really, totally gets it") then he probably could have been not racist and not homophobic. He HAD that awareness - you can see, right there in the book, the conflict between the recognition of these problems. Well, the recognition of racism, at least; not really where the homophobia is concerned, unfortunately. He clearly was not a fan of feminine men. One wonders, however, taking a critical look at all those big muscular strapping men (and the almost cursory way in which he describes beautiful women) if perhaps some of this homophobia was internally-focused. Just speculation you know.<br /><br />So The Diamond Throne is essentially decent - I read it all the way through, after all, and if you know me you know I'm happy to give up on shitty books - but it's bogged down by all these admittedly horrible problems. It's very weird to go back and read over his writing and see this being the case. I did know, even when I was 11 and reading the Belgariad, that the way he depicted the Murgos as the bad guys (with occasional exceptions, like Sephrenia and the Styrics) who were also non-white, was uncomfortable and not very good - but I didn't ever recognize how truly pervasive the race thing is. I don't like seeing this, because I grew up with his books and I loved that old series. But I can't pretend it's not there. It is, and it's really bad. That plus the homophobia, and I feel almost betrayed. <br />Notwithstanding all this, I have a truly inexplicable desire to read the next entry in the Elenium. I don't really know why, because I didn't love The Diamond Throne all that much, and I was seriously offended by the problems apparent in it. Maybe I'm still craving his pulpy, light, insufferably witty adventures, even after all this. Maybe I'm still an apologist for him. I certainly wouldn't be this nice with any other writer, I don't think. <br /><br />We'll see. In the meantime, this one gets a (fairly generous) 3 stars, because if nothing else he could write good female characters. His saving grace. I should re-read the Belgariad again... but I'm sort of terrified to do it, thinking what I might find. Being an adult sucks. </span>
  <a data-text-id="706499766189137136" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2549084042" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2549084042" />
    <a title="Roger Keays" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58137640-roger-keays"><img alt="Roger Keays" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478856994p2/58137640.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2549084042?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roger Keays" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roger Keays" href="/user/show/58137640-roger-keays">Roger Keays</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2549084042" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7887886668101248215">It's my own fault. I picked up <i>The Diamond Throne</i> because of my fond memories of reading <i>The Belgariad</i> as a teenager. In my own defense, I can only hope that Eddings' earlier work was better than his latter. Despite getting through four hundred pages of <i>The Diamond Throne</i>, I couldn't muster enough patience to get through the last few chapters. The characters are shallow, the plot circular, and the only notable themes are racism, religious tripe, and the type of avarice which you expect to find i</span>
  <span id="freeText7887886668101248215" style="display:none">It's my own fault. I picked up <i>The Diamond Throne</i> because of my fond memories of reading <i>The Belgariad</i> as a teenager. In my own defense, I can only hope that Eddings' earlier work was better than his latter. Despite getting through four hundred pages of <i>The Diamond Throne</i>, I couldn't muster enough patience to get through the last few chapters. The characters are shallow, the plot circular, and the only notable themes are racism, religious tripe, and the type of avarice which you expect to find in nearly all antagonists.<br /><br />The characters give away their shallow nature at the very beginning of the book with their impetuous behaviour and frequent sarcasm. Sparhawk, who is described as a sharp-witted, fearsome knight, behaves more like a spoiled brat. He gives money to a poor prostitute in one scene and a boil (by the use of magic) to a common courtier who annoys him in the next. His friends, Kalten and Kurik, only seem to be in the book to say something sarcastic in response to everything Sparhawk says.<br /><br />The dialogue between the characters more often than not feels like a conversation competition, which made me (correctly) suspect that the author was from the U.S.A. I think the intention was to bring some gritty modern culture into the world of fantasy. The result, however, was just some weird mix of modern banter with medieval formality which didn't work at all for me.<br /><br />Another weird writing device Eddings uses is to take real-world races and religions and import them into his fantasy world verbatim. The Elene Church is indistinguishable from the Christian Church, which makes you wonder how the author comes up with his ideas. I mean sure, art imitates life, but isn't the whole point of fantasy to escape from real life? Many of the races in his world are also proxies for real life (non-christian) races. Whether intentional or not, there does seem to be an odd religious prejudice throughout the book.<br /><br />The plot is dire. Boring, formulaic, and repetitive: Being followed, travel to the next city, attacked on the way, slaughter everyone, Sparhawk says something macho, Kalten says something sarcastic, Sparhawk says "I deserved that", arrive at the new city, stay with an old friend who owes them a favour, do what Sephrenia says... rinse and repeat. Nothing ever goes wrong for the good guys, and the bad guys are always thwarted easily. It's just boring.<br /><br />I think the real failing of this book is that although the author does well to <i>tell</i> us about his world, he fails to <i>show</i> us those very things. Sparhawk does not behave how he's described; Sephrenia is intended to be mysterious and powerful, but just comes off as powerless, bossy and fake. Even the most evil entities in the book, Azash and the Demork, inspire no fear or revulsion. Whatsmore, the urgency to save the Queen's life, which is supposed to be central to the plot, is simply <i>not conveyed</i>. The main characters spend their time hanging about in taverns, chasing up all their petty grievances, and can't continue their quest because they don't want to upset some other knights by leaving without them.<br /><br />The saving grace of <i>The Diamond Throne</i> is that is an easy, quick read. The use of language is also quite good, even though its effects are only weak. I can see now how adverbs weaken the impact of writing. For Buddha's sake, <i>show</i> us these characteristics, don't <i>tell</i> us.<br /><br />I expected too much from <i>The Diamond Throne</i>. I had thought Eddings was a master. Sadly, I've been given a reality check. It seems more appropriate to describe Eddings as the Mills and Boons of fantasy. Predictable and formulaic. Seeing as I'm unlikely to read any more of his work, I can only hope that I am wrong about that. There are too many other good books to read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7887886668101248215" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Bn/QAK9zetVCM6c6un6lXGwOzRNqI7Xyt0oh/kbkHC37sy0EJ9ZNGHFPlMiRq9v5Inejg4RVLaHVNhf8uQ0E4w==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('+LuzRkgxeceS3jcoXD6JIj5AtU3+K5CgohjVCGf6Dm0Fd05CwJROCqGiBNp36/eHcDnb3RBdCPPAZOMKmBMWow==')})
  }

Feist’s bestselling coming-of-age saga celebrates the fifteenth anniversary of its publication. Feist introduces a new generation of readers to his riveting novel of adventure and intrigue, revised and updated as he always meant it to be written. It is a work that explores strength and weakness, hope and fear, and what it means to be a man—in a kingdom where peace is the most precious commodity of all.If there were two more impetuous and carefree men in the Kingdom of the Isles, they had yet to be found. Twins Borric and Erland wore that mantle proudly, much to the chagrin of their father, Prince Arutha of Krondor. But their blissful youth has come to an end. Their uncle, the King, has produced no male children. Bypassing himself, Arutha names Borric, the eldest twin by seconds, the Royal Heir. As his brother, Erland will have his own great responsibilities to shoulder. To drive home their future roles, Arutha sends them as ambassadors to Kesh, the most feared nation in the world. Borric and Erland will be presented to the Queen of Kesh—the single most powerful ruler in the known world—at her Seventy-fifth Jubilee Anniversary. But they have not even left Krondor when an assassination attempt on Borric is thwarted. Aware that he is being provoked into war, Arutha does not rise to the bait. His sons’ journey will not be deterred, for nothing less than peace is riding on it. Yet there is to be no peace for the young princes. When their traveling party is ambushed, Borric disappears and is presumed dead—sending Erland into spirals of rage and grief as he is forced to navigate alone the court intrigues at Kesh. But unbeknownst to anyone, Borric lives and has escaped his captors. In a strange land, with a price on his head, Borric must use all his wits and stamina to find his way back to his brother. On separate paths, the two men—one a fugitive and one a future king—make their journey toward maturity, honor, and duty. For every step they take could sway the fragile peace of the land, as those who crave war rally against them—and become ever more daring.From the Hardcover edition.<\/span>\n  <a <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3944300647543552065">David Eddings was an American author who wrote several best-selling series of epic fantasy novels. David Eddings' wife, <b>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8732.Leigh_Eddings' title='Leigh Eddings' rel='nofollow noopener'>Leigh Eddings</a>
</b>, was an uncredited co-author on many of his early books, but he had later acknowledged that she contributed to them all.<br/><br/>David Eddings' first books (which were general fiction) sold moderately well. He later switched to writing epic fantasy, a field in which he a</span>
  <span id="freeText3944300647543552065" style="display:none">David Eddings was an American author who wrote several best-selling series of epic fantasy novels. David Eddings' wife, <b>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/8732.Leigh_Eddings" title="Leigh Eddings" rel="nofollow noopener">Leigh Eddings</a>
</b>, was an uncredited co-author on many of his early books, but he had later acknowledged that she contributed to them all.<br /><br />David Eddings' first books (which were general fiction) sold moderately well. He later switched to writing epic fantasy, a field in which he achieved great success. In a recent interview with sffworld.com, he said: "I don't take orders from readers."<br /><br />On January 26, 2007 it was reported that Eddings accidentally burned about a quarter of his office, next door to his house, along with his Excalibur sports car, and the original manuscripts for most of his novels. He was flushing the fuel tank of the car with water when he lit a piece of paper and threw into the puddle to test if it was still flammable.<br /><br />On February 28, 2007, David Eddings' wife, Leigh Eddings (born Judith Leigh Schall), died following a series of strokes. She was 69.<br /><br />David Eddings died on June 2, 2009 at the age of 77.</span>
